id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Stratospheric phenomena of Earth</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" of the largest <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> ozone hole ever recorded over the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a> in September 2006</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atmosphere_layers-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2688" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="1000" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Atmosphere_layers-en.svg/170px-Atmosphere_layers-en.svg.png" href="/wiki/File:Atmosphere_layers-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Layers of the atmosphere (not to scale). The Earth\'s ozone layer is mainly found in the lower portion of the stratosphere from approximately 20 to 30&#160;km (12 to 19&#160;mi).</div></div></div>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><caption>External audio</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Atmospheric_ozone.svg/420px-Atmospheric_ozone.svg.png 2x" width="210"/></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.sciencehistory.org/distillations/podcast/whatever-happened-to-the-ozone-hole" rel="nofollow">&#8220;Whatever Happened to the Ozone Hole?&#8221;</a>, <i>Distillations</i> Podcast Episode 230, April 17, 2018, <a href="/wiki/Science_History_Institute" title="Science History Institute">Science History Institute</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ozone depletion</b> describes two related events observed since the late 1970s: a steady lowering of about four percent in the total amount of <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth\'s</a> atmosphere (the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>), and a much larger springtime decrease in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratospheric" title="Stratospheric">stratospheric</a> ozone around Earth\'s polar regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO-20Q_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-20Q-1">[1]</a></sup> The latter phenomenon is referred to as the <b>ozone hole</b>. There are also springtime polar <a href="/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone_depletion_events" title="Tropospheric ozone depletion events">tropospheric ozone depletion events</a> in addition to these stratospheric events.\n</p><p>The main cause of ozone depletion and the ozone hole is manufactured chemicals, especially manufactured <a href="/wiki/Halocarbon" title="Halocarbon">halocarbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellants</a> and foam-<a href="/wiki/Blowing_agent" title="Blowing agent">blowing agents</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">chlorofluorocarbons</a> (CFCs), HCFCs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haloalkanes" title="Haloalkanes">halons</a>), referred to as <b>ozone-depleting substances</b> (<b>ODS</b>). These compounds are transported into the stratosphere by the winds after being emitted from the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Once in the stratosphere, they release halogen atoms through <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">photodissociation</a>, which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyze" title="Catalyze">catalyze</a> the breakdown of ozone (O<sub>3</sub>) into oxygen (O<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Both types of ozone depletion were observed to increase as emissions of halocarbons increased.\n</p><p>Ozone depletion and the ozone hole have generated worldwide concern over increased cancer risks and other negative effects. The ozone layer prevents most harmful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UVB" title="UVB">UVB</a> wavelengths of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light (UV light) from passing through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>. These wavelengths cause <a href="/wiki/Skin_cancer" title="Skin cancer">skin cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">sunburn</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cataracts" title="Cataracts">cataracts</a>, which were projected to increase dramatically as a result of thinning ozone, as well as harming plants and animals. These concerns led to the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> in 1987, which bans the production of CFCs, halons and other ozone-depleting chemicals.\n</p><p>The ban came into effect in 1989. Ozone levels stabilized by the mid-1990s and began to recover in the 2000s. Recovery is projected to continue over the next century, and the ozone hole is expected to reach pre-1980 levels by around 2075.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-recovery-projection_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-recovery-projection-4">[4]</a></sup> The Montreal Protocol is considered the most successful international environmental agreement to date.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_cycle_overview">Ozone cycle overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ozone cycle overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Three forms (or <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropes</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> are involved in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone-oxygen_cycle" title="Ozone-oxygen cycle">ozone-oxygen cycle</a>: oxygen atoms (O or atomic oxygen), oxygen gas (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> or diatomic oxygen), and ozone gas (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> or triatomic oxygen). Ozone is formed in the stratosphere when oxygen molecules photodissociate after intaking ultraviolet photons. This converts a single <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> into two atomic oxygen <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radicals</a>. The atomic oxygen radicals then combine with separate <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecules to create two <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> molecules. These ozone molecules absorb ultraviolet (UV) light, following which ozone splits into a molecule of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and an oxygen atom. The oxygen atom then joins up with an oxygen molecule to regenerate ozone. This is a continuing process that terminates when an oxygen atom recombines with an ozone molecule to make two <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecules.\n</p><p>O + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> &#8594; 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>\n</p><p>The total amount of ozone in the stratosphere is determined by a balance between photochemical production and recombination.\n</p><p>Ozone can be destroyed by a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radical</a> catalysts; the most important are the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radical</a> (OH&#183;), <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> radical (NO&#183;), <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> radical (Cl&#183;) and  <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> radical (Br&#183;). The dot is a notation to indicate that each species has an unpaired electron and is thus extremely reactive. All of these have both natural and man-made sources; at the present time, most of the OH&#183; and NO&#183; in the stratosphere is naturally occurring, but human activity has drastically increased the levels of chlorine and bromine. These elements are found in stable organic compounds, especially <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbons</a>, which can travel to the stratosphere without being destroyed in the troposphere due to their low reactivity. Once in the stratosphere, the Cl and Br atoms are released from the parent compounds by the action of ultraviolet light, e.g.\n</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap">CFCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> &#8594; Cl&#183; + &#183;<span class="chemf nowrap">CFCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>\n</p><p>Ozone is a highly reactive molecule that easily reduces to the more stable oxygen form with the assistance of a catalyst. Cl and Br atoms destroy ozone molecules through a variety of <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalytic</a> cycles. In the simplest example of such a cycle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> a chlorine atom reacts with an ozone molecule (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), taking an oxygen atom to form chlorine monoxide (ClO) and leaving an oxygen molecule (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). The ClO can react with a second molecule of ozone, releasing the chlorine atom and yielding two molecules of oxygen. The chemical shorthand for these gas-phase reactions is:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Cl&#183; + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> &#8594; ClO + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><br/> A chlorine atom removes an oxygen atom from an ozone molecule to make a ClO molecule</li>\n<li>ClO + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> &#8594; Cl&#183; + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><br/> This ClO can also remove an oxygen atom from another ozone molecule; the chlorine is free to repeat this two-step cycle</li></ul>\n<p>The overall effect is a decrease in the amount of ozone, though the rate of these processes can be decreased by the effects of <a href="/wiki/Null_cycle" title="Null cycle">null cycles</a>. More complicated mechanisms have also been discovered that lead to ozone destruction in the lower stratosphere.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ozone_cycle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Ozone_cycle.svg/220px-Ozone_cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Ozone_cycle.svg/330px-Ozone_cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Ozone_cycle.svg/440px-Ozone_cycle.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ozone_cycle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ozone cycle</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="721" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png/220px-TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png/330px-TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png/440px-TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TOMS_Global_Ozone_65N-65S.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global monthly average total ozone amount</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Min_ozone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="792" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Min_ozone.jpg/220px-Min_ozone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Min_ozone.jpg/330px-Min_ozone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Min_ozone.jpg/440px-Min_ozone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Min_ozone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lowest value of ozone measured by <a href="/wiki/Total_Ozone_Mapping_Spectrometer" title="Total Ozone Mapping Spectrometer">TOMS</a> each year in the ozone hole</div></div></div>\n<p>A single chlorine atom would continuously destroy ozone (thus a catalyst) for up to two years (the time scale for transport back down to the troposphere) were it not for reactions that remove them from this cycle by forming reservoir species such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> (HCl) and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine_nitrate" title="Chlorine nitrate">chlorine nitrate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">ClONO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). Bromine is even more efficient than chlorine at destroying ozone on a per atom basis, but there is much less bromine in the atmosphere at present. Both chlorine and bromine contribute significantly to overall ozone depletion. Laboratory studies have also shown that fluorine and iodine atoms participate in analogous catalytic cycles. However, fluorine atoms react rapidly with water and methane to form strongly bound <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">HF</a> in the Earth\'s stratosphere, while organic molecules containing iodine react so rapidly in the lower atmosphere that they do not reach the stratosphere in significant quantities.\n</p><p>A single chlorine atom is able to react with an average of 100,000 ozone molecules before it is removed from the catalytic cycle. This fact plus the amount of chlorine released into the atmosphere yearly by chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs) and hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HCFCs) demonstrates the danger of CFCs and HCFCs to the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_on_ozone_layer_depletion">Observations on ozone layer depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Observations on ozone layer depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The ozone hole is usually measured by reduction in the total <i>column ozone</i> above a point on the Earth\'s surface. This is normally expressed in <a href="/wiki/Dobson_unit" title="Dobson unit">Dobson units</a>; abbreviated as "DU". The most prominent decrease in ozone has been in the lower stratosphere. Marked decreases in column ozone in the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> spring and early summer compared to the early 1970s and before have been observed using instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Total_Ozone_Mapping_Spectrometer" title="Total Ozone Mapping Spectrometer">Total Ozone Mapping Spectrometer</a> (TOMS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reductions of up to 70 percent in the ozone column observed in the austral (southern hemispheric) spring over Antarctica and first reported in 1985 (Farman et al.) are continuing. Antarctic total column ozone in September and October have continued to be 40&#8211;50 percent lower than pre-ozone-hole values since the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO-20Q_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-20Q-1">[1]</a></sup> A gradual trend toward "healing" was reported in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-healing_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-healing-9">[9]</a></sup> In 2017, NASA announced that the ozone hole was the weakest since 1988 because of warm stratospheric conditions. It is expected to recover around 2070.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The amount lost is more variable year-to-year in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> than in the Antarctic. The greatest Arctic declines are in the winter and spring, reaching up to 30 percent when the stratosphere is coldest.\n</p><p>Reactions that take place on polar stratospheric clouds (PSCs) play an important role in enhancing ozone depletion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> PSCs form more readily in the extreme cold of the Arctic and Antarctic stratosphere. This is why ozone holes first formed, and are deeper, over Antarctica. Early models failed to take PSCs into account and predicted a gradual global depletion, which is why the sudden Antarctic ozone hole was such a surprise to many scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is more accurate to speak of ozone depletion in middle latitudes rather than holes. Total column ozone declined below pre-1980 values between 1980 and 1996 for mid-latitudes. In the northern mid-latitudes, it then increased from the minimum value by about two percent from 1996 to 2009 as regulations took effect and the amount of chlorine in the stratosphere decreased. In the Southern Hemisphere\'s mid-latitudes, total ozone remained constant over that time period. There are no significant trends in the tropics, largely because halogen-containing compounds have not had time to break down and release chlorine and bromine atoms at tropical latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO-20Q_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-20Q-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large volcanic eruptions have been shown to have substantial albeit uneven ozone-depleting effects, as observed with the 1991 eruption of Mt. Pinotubo in the Philippines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ozone depletion also explains much of the observed reduction in stratospheric and upper tropospheric temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wg1_223_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-wg1_223-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The source of the warmth of the stratosphere is the absorption of UV radiation by ozone, hence reduced ozone leads to cooling. Some stratospheric cooling is also predicted from increases in <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> such as <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></a></span> and CFCs themselves; however, the ozone-induced cooling appears to be dominant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Predictions of ozone levels remain difficult, but the precision of models\' predictions of observed values and the agreement among different modeling techniques have increased steadily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO-20Q_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-20Q-1">[1]</a></sup> The World Meteorological Organization Global Ozone Research and Monitoring Project&#8212;Report No. 44 comes out strongly in favor of the Montreal Protocol, but notes that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNEP" title="UNEP">UNEP</a> 1994 Assessment overestimated ozone loss for the 1994&#8211;1997 period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds_in_the_atmosphere">Compounds in the atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Compounds in the atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="CFCs_and_related_compounds_in_the_atmosphere">CFCs and related compounds in the atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: CFCs and related compounds in the atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs) and other halogenated ozone depleting substances (ODS) are mainly responsible for man-made chemical ozone depletion. The total amount of effective halogens (chlorine and bromine) in the stratosphere can be calculated and are known as the <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_effective_stratospheric_chlorine" title="Equivalent effective stratospheric chlorine">equivalent effective stratospheric chlorine</a> (EESC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>CFCs were invented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Midgley,_Jr." title="Thomas Midgley, Jr.">Thomas Midgley, Jr.</a> in the 1930s. They were used in <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> and cooling units, as <a href="/wiki/Aerosol_spray#Technology_(aerosol_propellants)" title="Aerosol spray">aerosol spray propellants</a> prior to the 1970s, and in the cleaning processes of delicate electronic equipment. They also occur as by-products of some chemical processes. No significant natural sources have ever been identified for these compounds&#8212;their presence in the atmosphere is due almost entirely to human manufacture. As mentioned above, when such ozone-depleting chemicals reach the stratosphere, they are dissociated by ultraviolet light to release chlorine atoms. The chlorine atoms act as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>, and each can break down tens of thousands of ozone molecules before being removed from the stratosphere. Given the longevity of CFC molecules, recovery times are measured in decades. It is calculated that a CFC molecule takes an average of about five to seven years to go from the ground level up to the upper atmosphere, and it can stay there for about a century, destroying up to one hundred thousand ozone molecules during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/1,1,1-Trichloro-2,2,2-trifluoroethane" title="1,1,1-Trichloro-2,2,2-trifluoroethane">1,1,1-Trichloro-2,2,2-trifluoroethane</a>, also known as CFC-113a, is one of four man-made chemicals newly discovered in the atmosphere by a team at the University of East Anglia. CFC-113a is the only known <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFC</a> whose abundance in the atmosphere is still growing. Its source remains a mystery, but illegal manufacturing is suspected by some.  CFC-113a seems to have been accumulating unabated since 1960. Between 2010 and 2012, emissions of the gas jumped by 45 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_modeling">Computer modeling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Computer modeling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Scientists have attributed ozone depletion to the increase of man-made (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anthropogenic" title="wikt:anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a>) halogen compounds from CFCs by combining observational data with computer models. These complex chemistry transport models (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SLIMCAT" title="SLIMCAT">SLIMCAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/CLaMS" title="CLaMS">CLaMS</a>&#8212;Chemical Lagrangian Model of the Stratosphere) work by combining measurements of chemicals and meteorological fields with chemical reaction rate constants. They identify key chemical reactions and transport processes that bring CFC <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">photolysis</a> products into contact with ozone.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_hole_and_its_causes">Ozone hole and its causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ozone hole and its causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uars_ozone_waves.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Uars_ozone_waves.jpg/220px-Uars_ozone_waves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Uars_ozone_waves.jpg/330px-Uars_ozone_waves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Uars_ozone_waves.jpg/440px-Uars_ozone_waves.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uars_ozone_waves.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ozone hole in North America during 1984 (abnormally warm reducing ozone depletion) and 1997 (abnormally cold resulting in increased seasonal depletion). Source: NASA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Antarctic ozone hole is an area of the Antarctic stratosphere in which the recent ozone levels have dropped to as low as 33 percent of their pre-1975 values. The ozone hole occurs during the Antarctic spring, from September to early December, as strong westerly winds start to circulate around the continent and create an atmospheric container. Within this <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">polar vortex</a>, over 50 percent of the lower stratospheric ozone is destroyed during the Antarctic spring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As explained above, the primary cause of ozone depletion is the presence of chlorine-containing source gases (primarily CFCs and related halocarbons). In the presence of UV light, these gases dissociate, releasing chlorine atoms, which then go on to catalyze ozone destruction. The Cl-catalyzed ozone depletion can take place in the gas phase, but it is dramatically enhanced in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Polar_stratospheric_cloud" title="Polar stratospheric cloud">polar stratospheric clouds</a> (PSCs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These polar stratospheric clouds form during winter, in the extreme cold. Polar winters are dark, consisting of three months without solar radiation (sunlight). The lack of sunlight contributes to a decrease in temperature and the polar vortex traps and chills air. Temperatures hover around or below &#8722;80&#160;&#176;C. These low temperatures form cloud particles. There are three types of PSC clouds&#8212;nitric acid trihydrate clouds, slowly cooling water-ice clouds, and rapid cooling water-ice (nacerous) clouds&#8212;provide surfaces for chemical reactions whose products will, in the spring lead to ozone destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemical</a> processes involved are complex but well understood. The key observation is that, ordinarily, most of the chlorine in the stratosphere resides in "reservoir" compounds, primarily chlorine nitrate (<span class="chemf nowrap">ClONO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) as well as stable end products such as HCl. The formation of end products essentially remove Cl from the ozone depletion process. The former sequester Cl, which can be later made available via absorption of light at shorter wavelengths than 400&#160;nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> During the Antarctic winter and spring, however, reactions on the surface of the polar stratospheric cloud particles convert these "reservoir" compounds into reactive free radicals (Cl and ClO). The process by which the clouds remove <span class="chemf nowrap">NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> from the stratosphere by converting it to nitric acid in the PSC particles, which then are lost by sedimentation is called denitrification. This prevents newly formed ClO from being converted back into <span class="chemf nowrap">ClONO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.\n</p><p>The role of sunlight in ozone depletion is the reason why the Antarctic ozone depletion is greatest during spring. During winter, even though PSCs are at their most abundant, there is no light over the pole to drive chemical reactions. During the spring, however, the sun comes out, providing energy to drive photochemical reactions and melt the polar stratospheric clouds, releasing considerable ClO, which drives the hole mechanism. Further warming temperatures near the end of spring break up the vortex around mid-December. As warm, ozone and <span class="chemf nowrap">NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>-rich air flows in from lower latitudes, the PSCs are destroyed, the enhanced ozone depletion process shuts down, and the ozone hole closes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most of the ozone that is destroyed is in the lower stratosphere, in contrast to the much smaller ozone depletion through homogeneous gas phase reactions, which occurs primarily in the upper stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interest_in_ozone_layer_depletion">Interest in ozone layer depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Interest in ozone layer depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Public misconceptions and misunderstandings of complex issues like the ozone depletion are common. The limited scientific knowledge of the public led to a confusion with global warming<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> or the perception of global warming as a subset of the "ozone hole".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\nIn the beginning, classical green NGOs refrained from using CFC depletion for campaigning, as they assumed the topic was too complicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-RG-34">[34]</a></sup> They became active much later, e.g. in Greenpeace\'s support for a CFC-free fridge produced by the former East German company <a href="/wiki/Volkseigener_Betrieb" title="Volkseigener Betrieb">VEB</a> dkk Scharfenstein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-RG-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spiegel_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spiegel-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The metaphors used in the CFC discussion (ozone shield, ozone hole) are not "exact" in the scientific sense. The "ozone hole" is more of a <i>depression</i>, less "a hole in the windshield". The ozone does not disappear through the layer, nor is there a uniform "thinning" of the ozone layer. However they resonated better with non-scientists and their concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ungar_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ungar-36">[36]</a></sup> The ozone hole was seen as a "hot issue" and imminent risk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAR_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAR-37">[37]</a></sup> as lay people feared severe personal consequences such as skin cancer, cataracts, damage to plants, and reduction of plankton populations in the ocean\'s photic zone. Not only on the policy level,  <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion_and_climate_change" title="Ozone depletion and climate change">ozone regulation compared to climate change</a> fared much better in public opinion. Americans voluntarily switched away from aerosol sprays before legislation was enforced, while climate change failed to achieve comparable concern and public action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ungar_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ungar-36">[36]</a></sup> The sudden recognition in 1985 that there was a substantial "hole" was widely reported in the press. The especially rapid ozone depletion in Antarctica had previously been dismissed as a measurement error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zehr94_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zehr94-38">[38]</a></sup> Scientific consensus was established after regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-RG-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the Antarctic ozone hole has a relatively small effect on global ozone, the hole has generated a great deal of public interest because:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Many have worried that ozone holes might start appearing over other areas of the globe, though to date the only other large-scale depletion is a smaller ozone "dimple" observed during the Arctic spring around the North Pole. Ozone at middle latitudes has declined, but by a much smaller extent (a decrease of about 4&#8211;5 percent).</li>\n<li>If stratospheric conditions become more severe (cooler temperatures, more clouds, more active chlorine), global ozone may decrease at a greater pace. Standard <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> theory predicts that the stratosphere will cool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>\n<li>When the Antarctic ozone hole breaks up each year, the ozone-depleted air drifts out into nearby regions. Decreases in the ozone level of up to 10 percent have been reported in New Zealand in the month following the breakup of the Antarctic ozone hole,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> with ultraviolet-B radiation intensities increasing by more than 15 percent since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences_of_ozone_layer_depletion">Consequences of ozone layer depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Consequences of ozone layer depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Since the ozone layer absorbs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_B" title="Ultraviolet B">UVB</a> ultraviolet light from the sun, ozone layer depletion increases surface UVB levels (all else equal), which could lead to damage, including increase in skin cancer. This was the reason for the Montreal Protocol. Although decreases in stratospheric ozone are well-tied to CFCs and to increases in surface UVB, there is no direct observational evidence linking ozone depletion to higher incidence of skin cancer and eye damage in human beings. This is partly because <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UVA</a>, which has also been implicated in some forms of skin cancer, is not absorbed by ozone, and because it is nearly impossible to control statistics for lifestyle changes over time.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_UV">Increased UV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Increased UV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Ozone, while a minority constituent in Earth\'s atmosphere, is responsible for most of the absorption of UVB radiation. The amount of UVB radiation that penetrates through the ozone layer <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">decreases exponentially</a> with the slant-path thickness and density of the layer. When stratospheric ozone levels decrease, higher levels of UVB reach the Earth&#8217;s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO-20Q_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-20Q-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> UV-driven phenolic formation in tree rings has dated the start of ozone depletion in northern latitudes to the late 1700s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2008, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecuadorian_Space_Agency" title="Ecuadorian Space Agency">Ecuadorian Space Agency</a> published a report called HIPERION. The study used ground instruments in Ecuador and the last 28 years\' data from 12 satellites of several countries, and found that the UV radiation reaching equatorial latitudes was far greater than expected, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_Index" title="UV Index">UV Index</a> climbing as high as 24 in <a href="/wiki/Quito" title="Quito">Quito</a>; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WHO" title="WHO">WHO</a> considers 11 as an extreme index and a great risk to health. The report concluded that depleted ozone levels around the mid-latitudes of the planet are already endangering large populations in these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Later, the CONIDA, the Peruvian Space Agency, published its own study, which yielded almost the same findings as the Ecuadorian study.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_effects">Biological effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biological effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The main public concern regarding the ozone hole has been the effects of increased surface UV radiation on human health. So far, ozone depletion in most locations has been typically a few percent and, as noted above, no direct evidence of health damage is available in most latitudes. If the high levels of depletion seen in the ozone hole were to be common across the globe, the effects could be substantially more dramatic. As the ozone hole over Antarctica has in some instances grown so large as to affect parts of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, environmentalists have been concerned that the increase in surface UV could be significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ozone depletion would magnify all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_sun_exposure" title="Health effects of sun exposure">effects of UV on human health</a>, both positive (including production of vitamin D) and negative (including sunburn, skin cancer, and cataracts). In addition, increased surface UV leads to increased tropospheric ozone, which is a health risk to humans.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Basal_and_squamous_cell_carcinomas">Basal and squamous cell carcinomas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Basal and squamous cell carcinomas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The most common forms of skin cancer in humans, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basal_cell_carcinoma" title="Basal cell carcinoma">basal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squamous_cell_carcinoma" title="Squamous cell carcinoma">squamous</a> cell carcinomas, have been strongly linked to UVB exposure. The mechanism by which UVB induces these cancers is well understood&#8212;absorption of UVB radiation causes the pyrimidine bases in the DNA molecule to form <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimers</a>, resulting in transcription errors when the DNA replicates. These cancers are relatively mild and rarely fatal, although the treatment of squamous cell carcinoma sometimes requires extensive reconstructive surgery. By combining epidemiological data with results of animal studies, scientists have estimated that every one percent decrease in long-term stratospheric ozone would increase the incidence of these cancers by two percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcrio.org-consequnces_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-gcrio.org-consequnces-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malignant_melanoma">Malignant melanoma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Malignant melanoma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Another form of skin cancer, malignant <a href="/wiki/Melanoma" title="Melanoma">melanoma</a>, is much less common but far more dangerous, being lethal in about 15&#8211;20 percent of the cases diagnosed. The relationship between malignant melanoma and ultraviolet exposure is not yet fully understood, but it appears that both UVB and UVA are involved. Because of this uncertainty, it is difficult to estimate the effect of ozone depletion on melanoma incidence. One study showed that a 10 percent increase in UVB radiation was associated with a 19 percent increase in melanomas for men and 16 percent for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> A study of people in <a href="/wiki/Punta_Arenas" title="Punta Arenas">Punta Arenas</a>, at the southern tip of <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, showed a 56 percent increase in melanoma and a 46 percent increase in nonmelanoma skin cancer over a period of seven years, along with decreased ozone and increased UVB levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cortical_cataracts">Cortical cataracts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cortical cataracts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Epidemiological studies suggest an association between ocular cortical cataracts and UVB exposure, using crude approximations of exposure and various cataract assessment techniques. A detailed assessment of ocular exposure to UVB was carried out in a study on Chesapeake Bay Watermen, where increases in average annual ocular exposure were associated with increasing risk of cortical opacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In this highly exposed group of predominantly white males, the evidence linking cortical opacities to sunlight exposure was the strongest to date. Based on these results, ozone depletion is predicted to cause hundreds of thousands of additional cataracts by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dobson2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dobson2005-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_tropospheric_ozone">Increased tropospheric ozone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Increased tropospheric ozone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Increased surface UV leads to increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropospheric" title="Tropospheric">tropospheric</a> ozone. Ground-level ozone is generally recognized to be a health risk, as ozone is toxic due to its strong <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidant</a> properties. The risks are particularly high for young children, the elderly, and those with asthma or other respiratory difficulties. At this time, ozone at ground level is produced mainly by the action of UV radiation on <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> gases from vehicle exhausts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_production_of_vitamin_D">Increased production of vitamin D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Increased production of vitamin D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a> is produced in the skin by ultraviolet light. Thus, higher UVB exposure raises human vitamin D in those deficient in it. Recent research (primarily since the Montreal Protocol) shows that many humans have less than optimal vitamin D levels. In particular, in the U.S. population, the lowest quarter of vitamin D (&lt;17.8&#160;ng/ml) were found using information from the National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey to be associated with an increase in all-cause mortality in the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> While blood level of vitamin D in excess of 100&#160;ng/ml appear to raise blood calcium excessively and to be associated with higher mortality, the body has mechanisms that prevent sunlight from producing vitamin D in excess of the body\'s requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_animals">Effects on animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Effects on animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A November 2010 report by scientists at the Institute of Zoology in London found that whales off the coast of California have shown a sharp rise in sun damage, and these scientists "fear that the thinning ozone layer is to blame".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The study photographed and took skin biopsies from over 150 whales in the Gulf of California and found "widespread evidence of epidermal damage commonly associated with acute and severe sunburn", having cells that form when the DNA is damaged by UV radiation. The findings suggest "rising UV levels as a result of ozone depletion are to blame for the observed skin damage, in the same way that human skin cancer rates have been on the increase in recent decades."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_crops">Effects on crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Effects on crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>An increase of UV radiation would be expected to affect crops. A number of economically important species of plants, such as <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, depend on <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> residing on their roots for the retention of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>. Cyanobacteria are sensitive to UV radiation and would be affected by its increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> "Despite mechanisms to reduce or repair the effects of increased ultraviolet radiation, plants have a limited ability to adapt to increased levels of UVB, therefore plant growth can be directly affected by UVB radiation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_policy">Public policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Public policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="932" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif/220px-Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif/330px-Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif/440px-Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NASA projections of stratospheric ozone concentrations if chlorofluorocarbons had not been banned</div></div></div>\n<p>The full extent of the damage that CFCs have caused to the ozone layer is not known and will not be known for decades; however, marked decreases in column ozone have already been observed. The Montreal and Vienna conventions were installed long before a scientific consensus was established or important uncertainties in the science field were being resolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-RG-34">[34]</a></sup> The ozone case was understood comparably well by lay persons as e.g. <i>Ozone shield</i> or <i>ozone hole</i> were useful "easy-to-understand bridging metaphors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ungar_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ungar-36">[36]</a></sup> Americans voluntarily switched away from aerosol sprays, resulting in a 50 percent sales loss even before legislation was enforced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ungar_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ungar-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a 1976 report by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">United States National Academy of Sciences</a> concluded that credible scientific evidence supported the ozone depletion hypothesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAS1976_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAS1976-59">[59]</a></sup> a few countries, including the United States, Canada, Sweden, Denmark, and Norway, moved to eliminate the use of CFCs in aerosol spray cans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrisette1989_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morrisette1989-60">[60]</a></sup> At the time this was widely regarded as a first step towards a more comprehensive regulation policy, but progress in this direction slowed in subsequent years, due to a combination of political factors (continued resistance from the halocarbon industry and a general change in attitude towards environmental regulation during the first two years of the Reagan administration) and scientific developments (subsequent National Academy assessments that indicated that the first estimates of the magnitude of ozone depletion had been overly large).\nA critical DuPont manufacturing patent for Freon was <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon#Regulation_and_DuPont" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">set to expire in 1979</a>. The United States banned the use of CFCs in aerosol cans in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrisette1989_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morrisette1989-60">[60]</a></sup> The European Community rejected proposals to ban CFCs in aerosol sprays, and in the U.S., CFCs continued to be used as refrigerants and for cleaning circuit boards. Worldwide CFC production fell sharply after the U.S. aerosol ban, but by 1986 had returned nearly to its 1976 level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrisette1989_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morrisette1989-60">[60]</a></sup> In 1993, <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a> Canada closed its CFC facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The U.S. government\'s attitude began to change again in 1983, when <a href="/wiki/William_Ruckelshaus" title="William Ruckelshaus">William Ruckelshaus</a> replaced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anne_M._Burford" title="Anne M. Burford">Anne M. Burford</a> as Administrator of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a>. Under Ruckelshaus and his successor, Lee Thomas, the EPA pushed for an international approach to halocarbon regulations. In 1985 twenty nations, including most of the major CFC producers, signed the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_the_Ozone_Layer" title="Vienna Convention for the Protection of the Ozone Layer">Vienna Convention for the Protection of the Ozone Layer</a>, which established a framework for negotiating international regulations on ozone-depleting substances. That same year, the discovery of the Antarctic ozone hole was announced, causing a revival in public attention to the issue. In 1987, representatives from 43 nations signed the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a>. Meanwhile, the halocarbon industry shifted its position and started supporting a protocol to limit CFC production. However, this shift was uneven with DuPont acting more quickly than its European counterparts. DuPont may have feared court action related to increased skin cancer, especially as the EPA had published a study in 1986 claiming that an additional 40 million cases and 800,000 cancer deaths were to be expected in the U.S. in the next 88 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The EU shifted its position as well after Germany gave up its defence of the CFC industry and started supporting moves towards regulation. Government and industry in France and the UK tried to defend their CFC producing industries even after the Montreal Protocol had been signed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At Montreal, the participants agreed to freeze production of CFCs at 1986 levels and to reduce production by 50 percent by 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrisette1989_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Morrisette1989-60">[60]</a></sup> After a series of scientific expeditions to the Antarctic produced convincing evidence that the ozone hole was indeed caused by chlorine and bromine from manmade organohalogens, the Montreal Protocol was strengthened at a 1990 meeting in London. The participants agreed to phase out CFCs and halons entirely (aside from a very small amount marked for certain "essential" uses, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asthma_inhaler" title="Asthma inhaler">asthma inhalers</a>) by 2000 in non-Article 5 countries and by 2010 in Article 5 (less developed) signatories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hist_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-hist-64">[64]</a></sup> At a 1992 meeting in Copenhagen, the phase-out date was moved up to 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hist_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-hist-64">[64]</a></sup> At the same meeting, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl_bromide" title="Methyl bromide">methyl bromide</a> (MeBr), a fumigant used primarily in agricultural production, was added to the list of controlled substances. For all substances controlled under the protocol, phaseout schedules were delayed for less developed (\'Article 5(1)\') countries, and phaseout in these countries was supported by transfers of expertise, technology, and money from non-Article 5(1) Parties to the Protocol. Additionally, exemptions from the agreed schedules could be applied for under the Essential Use Exemption (EUE) process for substances other than methyl bromide and under the Critical Use Exemption (CUE) process for methyl bromide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Civil society including especially NGOs, played critical roles at all stages of policy development leading up to the Vienna Conference, the Montreal Protocol, and in assessing compliance afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The major companies claimed that no alternatives to HFC existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace.org_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace.org-71">[71]</a></sup>  An ozone-safe hydrocarbon refrigerant was developed at a Hamburg technological institute in Germany, and in 1992 came to the attention of the non-governmental organization (NGO) Greenpeace.  Greenpeace was given the patent, called it "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Greenfreeze&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Greenfreeze (page does not exist)">Greenfreeze</a>," and left the patent as open source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>  The NGO then worked successfully first with a small and struggling company to market an appliance beginning in Europe, then Asia and later Latin America, receiving a 1997 UNEP award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecomall_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecomall-75">[75]</a></sup> By 1995, Germany had already made CFC refrigerators illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecomall_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-ecomall-75">[75]</a></sup>  Since 2004, corporations like Coca-Cola, Carlsberg, and IKEA have been forming a coalition to promote the ozone-safe Greenfreeze units. Production spread to companies like Electrolux, Bosch, and LG, with sales reaching some 300 million refrigerators by 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>  In Latin America, a domestic Argentinian company began Greenfreeze production in 2003, while the giant Bosch in Brazil began a year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> By 2013 it was being used by some 700 million refrigerators, making up about 40 percent of the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace.org_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace.org-71">[71]</a></sup>  In the U.S., however, change has been much slower.  To some extent, CFCs were being replaced by the less damaging hydrochlorofluorocarbons (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HCFC" title="HCFC">HCFCs</a>), although concerns remain regarding HCFCs also. In some applications, hydrofluorocarbons (<a href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">HFCs</a>) were being used to replace CFCs. HFCs, which contain no chlorine or bromine, do not contribute at all to ozone depletion although they are potent greenhouse gases. The best known of these compounds is probably HFC-134a (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R-134a" title="R-134a">R-134a</a>), which in the United States has largely replaced CFC-12 (<a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">R-12</a>) in automobile air conditioners. In laboratory analytics (a former "essential" use) the ozone depleting substances can be replaced with various other solvents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>  Chemical companies like Du Pont, whose representatives even disparaged Greenfreeze as "that German technology," maneuvered the EPA to block the technology in the U.S. until 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>  Ben &amp; Jerry\'s of Unilever and General Electric, spurred by Greenpeace, had expressed formal interest in 2008 which figured in the EPA\'s final approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More recently, policy experts have advocated for efforts to link ozone protection efforts to climate protection efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Many ODS are also greenhouse gases, some thousands of times more powerful agents of radiative forcing than carbon dioxide over the short and medium term. Thus policies protecting the ozone layer have had benefits in mitigating <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. In fact, the reduction of the radiative forcing due to ODS probably masked the true level of climate change effects of other GHGs, and was responsible for the "slow down" of global warming from the mid-90s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Policy decisions in one arena affect the costs and effectiveness of environmental improvements in the other.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ODS_requirements_in_the_marine_industry">ODS requirements in the marine industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: ODS requirements in the marine industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Maritime_Organization" title="International Maritime Organization">IMO</a> has amended <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MARPOL" title="MARPOL">MARPOL</a> Annex VI Regulation 12 regarding ozone depleting substances. As from July 1, 2010, all vessels where MARPOL Annex VI is applicable should have a list of equipment using ozone depleting substances. The list should include name of ODS, type and location of equipment, quantity in kg and date. All changes since that date should be recorded in an ODS Record book on board recording all intended or unintended releases to the atmosphere. Furthermore, new ODS supply or landing to shore facilities should be recorded as well.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prospects_of_ozone_depletion">Prospects of ozone depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Prospects of ozone depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="574" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg/220px-Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg/330px-Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg/440px-Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ozone_hole_recovery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ozone levels stabilized in the 1990s following the Montreal Protocol, and have started to recover. They are projected to reach pre-1980 levels before 2075.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-recovery-projection_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-recovery-projection-4">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="936" data-file-width="1398" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png/220px-TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png/330px-TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png/440px-TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TotalCl_NOAA-HATS.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ozone-depleting gas trends</div></div></div>\n<p>Since the adoption and strengthening of the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> has led to reductions in the emissions of CFCs, atmospheric concentrations of the most-significant compounds have been declining. These substances are being gradually removed from the atmosphere; since peaking in 1994, the Effective Equivalent Chlorine (EECl) level in the atmosphere had dropped about 10 percent by 2008. The decrease in ozone-depleting chemicals has also been significantly affected by a decrease in <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>-containing chemicals. The data suggest that substantial natural sources exist for atmospheric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl_bromide" title="Methyl bromide">methyl bromide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Br</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO-20Q_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-20Q-1">[1]</a></sup> The phase-out of CFCs means that <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>), which is not covered by the Montreal Protocol, has become the most highly emitted ozone-depleting substance and is expected to remain so throughout the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2005 <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> review of ozone observations and model calculations concluded that the global amount of ozone has now approximately stabilized. Although considerable variability is expected from year to year, including in polar regions where depletion is largest, the ozone layer is expected to begin to recover in coming decades due to declining ozone-depleting substance concentrations, assuming full compliance with the Montreal Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spm_ozone_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-spm_ozone-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Antarctic ozone hole is expected to continue for decades. Ozone concentrations in the lower stratosphere over Antarctica will increase by 5&#8211;10 percent by 2020 and return to pre-1980 levels by about 2060&#8211;2075. This is 10&#8211;25 years later than predicted in earlier assessments, because of revised estimates of atmospheric concentrations of ozone-depleting substances, including a larger predicted future usage in developing countries. Another factor that may prolong ozone depletion is the drawdown of nitrogen oxides from above the stratosphere due to changing wind patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> A gradual trend toward "healing" was reported in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-healing_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-healing-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_history">Research history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Research history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ozone%E2%80%93oxygen_cycle" title="Ozone&#8211;oxygen cycle">Ozone&#8211;oxygen cycle</a></div>\n<p>The basic physical and chemical processes that lead to the formation of an ozone layer in the Earth\'s stratosphere were discovered by <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Chapman_(mathematician)" title="Sydney Chapman (mathematician)">Sydney Chapman</a> in 1930. Short-wavelength UV radiation splits an oxygen (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) molecule into two oxygen (O) atoms, which then combine with other oxygen molecules to form ozone. Ozone is removed when an oxygen atom and an ozone molecule "recombine" to form two oxygen molecules, i.e. O + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> &#8594; 2<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. In the 1950s, David Bates and Marcel Nicolet presented evidence that various free radicals, in particular hydroxyl (OH) and nitric oxide (NO), could catalyze this recombination reaction, reducing the overall amount of ozone.
