These free radicals were known to be present in the stratosphere, and so were regarded as part of the natural balance&#8212;it was estimated that in their absence, the ozone layer would be about twice as thick as it currently is.\n</p><p>In 1970 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Crutzen" title="Paul Crutzen">Paul Crutzen</a> pointed out that emissions of <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>), a stable, long-lived gas produced by soil bacteria, from the Earth\'s surface could affect the amount of <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a> (NO) in the stratosphere. Crutzen showed that nitrous oxide lives long enough to reach the stratosphere, where it is converted into NO. Crutzen then noted that increasing use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilizers" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a> might have led to an increase in nitrous oxide emissions over the natural background, which would in turn result in an increase in the amount of NO in the stratosphere. Thus human activity could affect the stratospheric ozone layer. In the following year, Crutzen and (independently) Harold Johnston suggested that NO emissions from <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_transport" title="Supersonic transport">supersonic passenger aircraft</a>, which would fly in the lower stratosphere, could also deplete the ozone layer. However, more recent analysis in 1995 by David W. Fahey, an atmospheric scientist at the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>, found that the drop in ozone would be from 1&#8211;2 percent if a fleet of 500 supersonic passenger aircraft were operated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This, Fahey expressed, would not be a showstopper for advanced supersonic passenger aircraft development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Rowland.E2.80.93Molina_hypothesis"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rowland&#8211;Molina_hypothesis">Rowland&#8211;Molina hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Rowland&#8211;Molina hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1974 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_Sherwood_Rowland" title="Frank Sherwood Rowland">Frank Sherwood Rowland</a>, Chemistry Professor at the University of California at Irvine, and his postdoctoral associate <a href="/wiki/Mario_J._Molina" title="Mario J. Molina">Mario J. Molina</a> suggested that long-lived organic halogen compounds, such as CFCs, might behave in a similar fashion as Crutzen had proposed for nitrous oxide. <a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a> had recently discovered, during a cruise in the South Atlantic in 1971, that almost all of the CFC compounds manufactured since their invention in 1930 were still present in the atmosphere. Molina and Rowland concluded that, like <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>, the CFCs would reach the stratosphere where they would be dissociated by UV light, releasing chlorine atoms. A year earlier, Richard Stolarski and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Cicerone" title="Ralph Cicerone">Ralph Cicerone</a> at the University of Michigan had shown that Cl is even more efficient than NO at catalyzing the destruction of ozone. Similar conclusions were reached by <a href="/wiki/Michael_McElroy_(scientist)" title="Michael McElroy (scientist)">Michael McElroy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Wofsy" title="Steven Wofsy">Steven Wofsy</a> at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>. Neither group, however, had realized that CFCs were a potentially large source of stratospheric chlorine&#8212;instead, they had been investigating the possible effects of HCl emissions from the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>, which are very much smaller.\n</p><p>The Rowland&#8211;Molina hypothesis was strongly disputed by representatives of the aerosol and halocarbon industries. The Chair of the Board of <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a> was quoted as saying that ozone depletion theory is "a science fiction tale &#8230; a load of rubbish &#8230; utter nonsense".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace-ozone_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace-ozone-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Abplanalp" title="Robert Abplanalp">Robert Abplanalp</a>, the President of Precision Valve Corporation (and inventor of the first practical aerosol spray can valve), wrote to the Chancellor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UC_Irvine" title="UC Irvine">UC Irvine</a> to complain about Rowland\'s public statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Nevertheless, within three years most of the basic assumptions made by Rowland and Molina were confirmed by laboratory measurements and by direct observation in the stratosphere. The concentrations of the source gases (CFCs and related compounds) and the chlorine reservoir species (HCl and <span class="chemf nowrap">ClONO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) were measured throughout the stratosphere, and demonstrated that CFCs were indeed the major source of stratospheric chlorine, and that nearly all of the CFCs emitted would eventually reach the stratosphere. Even more convincing was the measurement, by James G. Anderson and collaborators, of chlorine monoxide (ClO) in the stratosphere. ClO is produced by the reaction of Cl with ozone&#8212;its observation thus demonstrated that Cl radicals not only were present in the stratosphere but also were actually involved in destroying ozone. McElroy and Wofsy extended the work of Rowland and Molina by showing that bromine atoms were even more effective catalysts for ozone loss than chlorine atoms and argued that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">brominated organic compounds</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">halons</a>, widely used in fire extinguishers, were a potentially large source of stratospheric bromine. In 1976 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">United States National Academy of Sciences</a> released a report concluding that the ozone depletion hypothesis was strongly supported by the scientific evidence.  In response the United States, Canada and Norway banned the use of CFCs in <a href="/wiki/Aerosol_spray" title="Aerosol spray">aerosol spray cans</a> in 1978. Early estimates were that, if CFC production continued at 1977 levels, the total atmospheric ozone would after a century or so reach a steady state, 15 to 18 percent below normal levels. By 1984, when better evidence on the speed of critical reactions was available, this estimate was changed to 5 to 9 percent steady-state depletion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAS_report_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAS_report-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crutzen, Molina, and Rowland were awarded the 1995 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> for their work on stratospheric ozone.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antarctic_ozone_hole">Antarctic ozone hole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Antarctic ozone hole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The discovery of the Antarctic "ozone hole" by <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Survey" title="British Antarctic Survey">British Antarctic Survey</a> scientists <a href="/wiki/Joe_Farman" title="Joe Farman">Farman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_G._Gardiner_(meteorologist)" title="Brian G. Gardiner (meteorologist)">Gardiner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jon_Shanklin" title="Jon Shanklin">Shanklin</a> (first reported in a paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> in May 1985<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>) came as a shock to the scientific community, because the observed decline in polar ozone was far larger than anyone had anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zehr94_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zehr94-38">[38]</a></sup> Satellite measurements showing massive depletion of ozone around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_pole" title="South pole">south pole</a> were becoming available at the same time. However, these were initially rejected as unreasonable by data quality control algorithms (they were filtered out as errors since the values were unexpectedly low); the ozone hole was detected only in satellite data when the raw data was reprocessed following evidence of ozone depletion in <i>in situ</i> observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-63">[63]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> was rerun without the flags, the ozone hole was seen as far back as 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Susan_Solomon" title="Susan Solomon">Susan Solomon</a>, an atmospheric chemist at the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA), proposed that chemical reactions on polar stratospheric clouds (PSCs) in the cold Antarctic stratosphere caused a massive, though localized and seasonal, increase in the amount of chlorine present in active, ozone-destroying forms. The polar stratospheric clouds in Antarctica are only formed when there are very low temperatures, as low as &#8722;80&#160;&#176;C, and early spring conditions. In such conditions the ice crystals of the cloud provide a suitable surface for conversion of unreactive chlorine compounds into reactive chlorine compounds, which can deplete ozone easily.\n</p><p>Moreover, the polar vortex formed over <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> is very tight and the reaction occurring on the surface of the cloud crystals is far different from when it occurs in atmosphere. These conditions have led to ozone hole formation in Antarctica. This hypothesis was decisively confirmed, first by laboratory measurements and subsequently by direct measurements, from the ground and from high-altitude airplanes, of very high concentrations of chlorine monoxide (ClO) in the Antarctic stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Alternative hypotheses, which had attributed the ozone hole to variations in solar UV radiation or to changes in atmospheric circulation patterns, were also tested and shown to be untenable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, analysis of ozone measurements from the worldwide network of ground-based Dobson spectrophotometers led an international panel to conclude that the ozone layer was in fact being depleted, at all latitudes outside of the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-15">[15]</a></sup> These trends were confirmed by satellite measurements. As a consequence, the major halocarbon-producing nations agreed to phase out production of CFCs, halons, and related compounds, a process that was completed in 1996.\n</p><p>Since 1981 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a>, under the auspices of the World Meteorological Organization, has sponsored a series of technical reports on the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Assessment_of_Ozone_Depletion" title="Scientific Assessment of Ozone Depletion">Scientific Assessment of Ozone Depletion</a>, based on satellite measurements. The 2007 report showed that the hole in the ozone layer was recovering and the smallest it had been for about a decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\nThe 2010 report found, "Over the past decade, global ozone and ozone in the Arctic and Antarctic regions is no longer decreasing but is not yet increasing. The ozone layer outside the Polar regions is projected to recover to its pre-1980 levels some time before the middle of this century. In contrast, the springtime ozone hole over the Antarctic is expected to recover much later."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\nIn 2012, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA" title="NOAA">NOAA</a> and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reported "Warmer air temperatures high above the Antarctic led to the second smallest season ozone hole in 20 years averaging 17.9 million square kilometres. The hole reached its maximum size for the season on Sept 22, stretching to 21.2 million square kilometres."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> A gradual trend toward "healing" was reported in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-healing_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-healing-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The hole in the Earth\'s ozone layer over the South Pole has affected atmospheric circulation in the Southern Hemisphere all the way to the equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The ozone hole has influenced atmospheric circulation all the way to the tropics and increased rainfall at low, subtropical latitudes in the Southern Hemisphere.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_ozone_hole">Arctic ozone hole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Arctic ozone hole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On March 3, 2005, the journal <i>Nature</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> published an article linking 2004\'s unusually large Arctic ozone hole to solar wind activity.\n</p><p>On March 15, 2011, a record ozone layer loss was observed, with about half of the ozone present over the Arctic having been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The change was attributed to increasingly cold winters in the Arctic stratosphere at an altitude of approximately 20&#160;km (12&#160;mi), a change associated with global warming in a relationship that is still under investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-verge-106">[106]</a></sup> By March 25, the ozone loss had become the largest compared to that observed in all previous winters with the possibility that it would become an ozone hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euractiv1_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-euractiv1-108">[108]</a></sup> This would require that the quantities of ozone to fall below 200 Dobson units, from the 250 recorded over central Siberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euractiv1_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-euractiv1-108">[108]</a></sup> It is predicted that the thinning layer would affect parts of Scandinavia and Eastern Europe on March 30&#8211;31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euractiv1_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-euractiv1-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 2, 2011, a study was published in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>, which said that between December 2010 and March 2011 up to 80 percent of the ozone in the atmosphere at about 20 kilometres (12&#160;mi) above the surface was destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-109">[109]</a></sup> The level of ozone depletion was severe enough that scientists said it could be compared to the ozone hole that forms over Antarctica every winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-109">[109]</a></sup> According to the study, "for the first time, sufficient loss occurred to reasonably be described as an Arctic ozone hole."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-109">[109]</a></sup> The study analyzed data from the <a href="/wiki/Aura_(satellite)" title="Aura (satellite)">Aura</a> and <a href="/wiki/CALIPSO" title="CALIPSO">CALIPSO</a> satellites, and determined that the larger-than-normal ozone loss was due to an unusually long period of cold weather in the Arctic, some 30 days more than typical, which allowed for more ozone-destroying chlorine compounds to be created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-press_release_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-press_release-110">[110]</a></sup> According to Lamont Poole, a co-author of the study, cloud and aerosol particles on which the chlorine compounds are found "were abundant in the Arctic until mid March 2011&#8212;much later than usual&#8212;with average amounts at some altitudes similar to those observed in the Antarctic, and dramatically larger than the near-zero values seen in March in most Arctic winters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-press_release_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-press_release-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tibet_ozone_hole">Tibet ozone hole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Tibet ozone hole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As winters that are colder are more affected, at times there is an ozone hole over Tibet. In 2006, a 2.5 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_kilometer" title="Square kilometer">square kilometer</a> ozone hole was detected over Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Also again in 2011 an ozone hole appeared over mountainous regions of <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a>, along with an unprecedented hole over the Arctic, though the Tibet one is far less intense than the ones over the Arctic or Antarctic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_depletion_by_storm_clouds">Potential depletion by storm clouds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Potential depletion by storm clouds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Research in 2012 showed that the same process that produces the ozone hole over Antarctica occurs over summer storm clouds in the United States, and thus may be destroying ozone there as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_depletion_and_global_warming">Ozone depletion and global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ozone depletion and global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion_and_climate_change" title="Ozone depletion and climate change">Ozone depletion and global warming</a></div>\n<p>Among others, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Watson_(scientist)" title="Robert Watson (scientist)">Robert Watson</a> had a role in the science assessment and in the regulation efforts of <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion_and_climate_change" title="Ozone depletion and climate change">ozone depletion and global warming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-RG-34">[34]</a></sup> Prior to the 1980s, the EU, NASA, NAS, UNEP, WMO and the British government had dissenting scientific reports and Watson played a role in the process of unified assessments. Based on the experience with the ozone case, the IPCC started to work on a unified reporting and science assessment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-RG-34">[34]</a></sup>  to reach a consensus to provide the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Summary_for_Policymakers" title="IPCC Summary for Policymakers">IPCC Summary for Policymakers</a>.\n</p><p>There are various areas of linkage between ozone depletion and global warming science:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/370px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/555px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/740px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a> from various greenhouse gases and other sources</div></div></div>\n<ul><li>The same <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> radiative forcing that produces global warming is expected to cool the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc2007_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc2007-115">[115]</a></sup> This cooling, in turn, is expected to produce a relative <i>increase</i> in ozone (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) depletion in polar area and the frequency of ozone holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Conversely, ozone depletion represents a radiative forcing of the climate system. There are two opposing effects: Reduced ozone causes the stratosphere to absorb less solar radiation, thus cooling the stratosphere while warming the troposphere; the resulting colder stratosphere emits less long-wave radiation downward, thus cooling the troposphere. Overall, the cooling dominates; the IPCC concludes  "<i>observed stratospheric <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone"><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a> losses over the past two decades have caused a negative forcing of the surface-troposphere system</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wg1_223_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-wg1_223-17">[17]</a></sup> of about &#8722;0.15&#160;&#177; 0.10 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> per square meter (W/m<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spm_ozone_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-spm_ozone-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>One of the strongest predictions of the greenhouse effect is that the stratosphere will cool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc2007_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc2007-115">[115]</a></sup> Although this cooling has been observed, it is not trivial to separate the effects of changes in the concentration of greenhouse gases and ozone depletion since both will lead to cooling. However, this can be done by numerical stratospheric modeling. Results from the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_Fluid_Dynamics_Laboratory" title="Geophysical Fluid Dynamics Laboratory">Geophysical Fluid Dynamics Laboratory</a> show that above 20&#160;km (12&#160;mi), the greenhouse gases dominate the cooling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></li>\n<li>As noted under \'Public Policy\', ozone depleting chemicals are also often greenhouse gases. The increases in concentrations of these chemicals have produced 0.34&#160;&#177; 0.03&#160;W/m<sup>2</sup> of radiative forcing, corresponding to about 14 percent of the total radiative forcing from increases in the concentrations of well-mixed greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spm_ozone_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-spm_ozone-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The long term modeling of the process, its measurement, study, design of theories and testing take decades to document, gain wide acceptance, and ultimately become the dominant paradigm. Several theories about the destruction of ozone were hypothesized in the 1980s, published in the late 1990s, and are currently being investigated. Dr Drew Schindell, and Dr Paul Newman, NASA Goddard, proposed a theory in the late 1990s, using computational modeling methods to model ozone destruction, that accounted for 78 percent of the ozone destroyed. Further refinement of that model accounted for 89 percent of the ozone destroyed, but pushed back the estimated recovery of the ozone hole from 75 years to 150 years. (An important part of that model is the lack of stratospheric flight due to depletion of fossil fuels.)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Misconceptions">Misconceptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Misconceptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CFC_weight">CFC weight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: CFC weight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since CFC molecules are heavier than air (nitrogen or oxygen), it is commonly believed that the CFC molecules cannot reach the stratosphere in significant amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> However, atmospheric gases are not sorted by weight; the forces of wind can fully mix the gases in the atmosphere. Lighter CFCs are evenly distributed throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbosphere" title="Turbosphere">turbosphere</a> and reach the upper atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> although some of the heavier CFCs are not evenly distributed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Percentage_of_man-made_chlorine">Percentage of man-made chlorine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Percentage of man-made chlorine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="669" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png/300px-Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png/450px-Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png/600px-Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sources_stratospheric_chlorine.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sources of stratospheric chlorine</div></div></div>\n<p>Another misconception is that "it is generally accepted that natural sources of tropospheric chlorine are four to five times larger than man-made ones." While strictly true, <i>tropospheric</i> chlorine is irrelevant; it is <i>stratospheric</i> chlorine that affects ozone depletion. Chlorine from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_spray" title="Salt spray">ocean spray</a> is soluble and thus is washed by rainfall before it reaches the stratosphere. CFCs, in contrast, are insoluble and long-lived, allowing them to reach the stratosphere. In the lower atmosphere, there is much more chlorine from CFCs and related <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">haloalkanes</a> than there is in HCl from salt spray, and in the stratosphere halocarbons are dominant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Only methyl chloride, which is one of these halocarbons, has a mainly natural source,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and it is responsible for about 20 percent of the chlorine in the stratosphere; the remaining 80 percent comes from manmade sources.\n</p><p>Very violent volcanic eruptions can inject HCl into the stratosphere, but researchers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O3F4_4_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-O3F4_4-123">[123]</a></sup> have shown that the contribution is not significant compared to that from CFCs.\nA similar erroneous assertion is that soluble halogen compounds from the volcanic plume of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus" title="Mount Erebus">Mount Erebus</a> on Ross Island, Antarctica are a major contributor to the Antarctic ozone hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O3F4_4_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-O3F4_4-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nevertheless, a 2015 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> showed that the role of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus" title="Mount Erebus">Mount Erebus</a> volcano in the Antarctic ozone depletion was probably underestimated. Based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCEP/NCAR_reanalysis" title="NCEP/NCAR reanalysis">NCEP/NCAR reanalysis</a> data over the last 35 years and by using the NOAA <a href="/wiki/HYSPLIT" title="HYSPLIT">HYSPLIT</a> trajectory model, researchers showed that Erebus volcano gas emissions (including <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> (HCl)) can reach the Antarctic stratosphere via high-latitude cyclones and then the <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">polar vortex</a>. Depending on Erebus volcano activity, the additional annual HCl mass entering the stratosphere from Erebus varies from 1.0 to 14.3 kt.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_observation">First observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: First observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.M.B._Dobson" title="G.M.B. Dobson">G.M.B. Dobson</a> mentioned that when springtime ozone levels in the Antarctic over <a href="/wiki/Halley_Research_Station" title="Halley Research Station">Halley Bay</a> were first measured in 1956, he was surprised to find that they were ~320 DU, or about 150 DU below spring Arctic levels of ~450 DU. These were at that time the only known Antarctic ozone values available. What Dobson describes is essentially the <i>baseline</i> from which the ozone hole is measured: actual ozone hole values are in the 150&#8211;100 DU range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The discrepancy between the Arctic and Antarctic noted by Dobson was primarily a matter of timing: during the Arctic spring ozone levels rose smoothly, peaking in April, whereas in the Antarctic they stayed approximately constant during early spring, rising abruptly in November when the polar vortex broke down.\n</p><p>The behavior seen in the Antarctic ozone hole is completely different. Instead of staying constant, early springtime ozone levels suddenly drop from their already low winter values, by as much as 50 percent, and normal values are not reached again until December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_of_hole">Location of hole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Location of hole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some people thought that the ozone hole should be above the sources of CFCs. However, CFCs are well mixed globally in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>. The reason for occurrence of the ozone hole above Antarctica is not because there are more CFCs concentrated but because the low temperatures help form polar stratospheric clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In fact, there are findings of significant and localized "ozone holes" above other parts of the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (March 2018)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Ozone_Day">World Ozone Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: World Ozone Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1994, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> voted to designate September 16 as the <a href="/wiki/International_Day_for_the_Preservation_of_the_Ozone_Layer" title="International Day for the Preservation of the Ozone Layer">International Day for the Preservation of the Ozone Layer</a>, or "World Ozone Day", to commemorate the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> on that date in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Warming_Map.jpg"><img alt="Global Warming Map.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="596" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="24" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Global_Warming_Map.jpg/32px-Global_Warming_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Global_Warming_Map.jpg/48px-Global_Warming_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Global_Warming_Map.jpg/64px-Global_Warming_Map.jpg 2x" width="32"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Global_warming" title="Portal:Global warming">Global warming portal</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aegopodium_podagraria1_ies.jpg"><img alt="Aegopodium podagraria1 ies.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="800"
